<p><b>Warner Bros. Entertainment Inc.</b> (known professionally as <b>Warner Bros. Pictures</b>, often referred to as <b>Warner Bros.</b> and <b>Warner Brothers</b> and abbreviated as <b>WB</b>) is an American <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Entertainment_industry" title="Entertainment industry">entertainment company</a>, <a href="/wiki/Film_studio" title="Film studio">film studio</a> and <a href="/wiki/Film_distributor" title="Film distributor">film distributor</a> that is a division of <a href="/wiki/Time_Warner" title="Time Warner">Time Warner</a> and is headquartered in <a href="/wiki/Burbank,_California" title="Burbank, California">Burbank, California</a>. It is one of the <a href="/wiki/Major_film_studio" title="Major film studio">"Big Six"</a> major American film studios.</p>
<p>Warner Bros. is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Motion_Picture_Association_of_America" title="Motion Picture Association of America">Motion Picture Association of America</a> (MPAA) and is the third largest movie studio in the world after <a href="/wiki/Paramount_Pictures" title="Paramount Pictures">Paramount Pictures</a> and <a href="/wiki/Universal_Studios" title="Universal Studios">Universal Studios</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Founding">Founding</span></h3>
<p>The company's name originated from the four founding Warner brothers (born <i>Wonskolaser</i> or <i>Wonsal</i> before Anglicization):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Harry_Warner" title="Harry Warner">Harry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Albert_Warner" title="Albert Warner">Albert</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sam_Warner" title="Sam Warner">Sam</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jack_L._Warner" title="Jack L. Warner">Jack</a> Warner. They emigrated as small children with their parents to Canada from <a href="/wiki/Krasnosielc" title="Krasnosielc">Krasnosielc</a> which was located in the part of <a href="/wiki/Congress_Poland" title="Congress Poland">Congress Poland</a> that had been subjugated to the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a> following the eighteenth-century <a href="/wiki/Partitions_of_Poland" title="Partitions of Poland">Partitions of Poland</a> near present-day <a href="/wiki/Ostro%C5%82%C4%99ka" title="Ostrołęka">Ostrołęka</a>.</p>
<p>Jack, the youngest, was born in London, Ontario. The three elder brothers began in the <a href="/wiki/Movie_theater" title="Movie theater">movie theater</a> business, having acquired a <a href="/wiki/Movie_projector" title="Movie projector">movie projector</a> with which they showed films in the mining towns of <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a>. In the beginning,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Sam and Albert Warner invested $150 to present <i><a href="/wiki/Life_of_an_American_Fireman" title="Life of an American Fireman">Life of an American Fireman</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Great_Train_Robbery_(1903_film)" title="The Great Train Robbery (1903 film)">The Great Train Robbery</a></i>. They opened their first theater, the Cascade, in <a href="/wiki/New_Castle,_Pennsylvania" title="New Castle, Pennsylvania">New Castle, Pennsylvania</a>, in 1903.</p>
<p>When the original building was in danger of being demolished, the modern Warner Bros. called the current building owners, and arranged to save it. The owners noted people across the country had asked them to protect it for its historical significance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1904, the Warners founded the <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh" title="Pittsburgh">Pittsburgh</a>-based Duquesne Amusement &amp; Supply Company,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-firstwarnertheatre.com_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-firstwarnertheatre.com-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> to distribute films. In 1912, Harry Warner hired an auditor named <a href="/wiki/Paul_Ashley_Chase" title="Paul Ashley Chase">Paul Ashley Chase</a>. By the time of <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> they had begun producing films. In 1918 they opened the <a href="/wiki/Old_Warner_Brothers_Studio" title="Old Warner Brothers Studio">first Warner Bros. studio</a> on <a href="/wiki/Sunset_Boulevard" title="Sunset Boulevard">Sunset Boulevard</a> in Hollywood. Sam and Jack produced the pictures, while Harry and Albert, along with their auditor and now controller Chase, handled finance and distribution in New York City. During World War I their first nationally syndicated film, <i>My Four Years in Germany,</i> based on a popular book by former ambassador <a href="/wiki/James_W._Gerard" title="James W. Gerard">James W. Gerard</a>, was released. On April 4, 1923, with help from money loaned to Harry by his banker Motley Flint,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-p77_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-p77-12">[12]</a></sup> they formally incorporated as <b>Warner Brothers Pictures, Incorporated</b>. (As late as the 1960s, Warner Bros. claimed 1905 as its founding date.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Open_Your_Eyes_(1919)_-_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="437" data-file-width="580" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Open_Your_Eyes_%281919%29_-_1.jpg/250px-Open_Your_Eyes_%281919%29_-_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Open_Your_Eyes_%281919%29_-_1.jpg/375px-Open_Your_Eyes_%281919%29_-_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Open_Your_Eyes_%281919%29_-_1.jpg/500px-Open_Your_Eyes_%281919%29_-_1.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Open_Your_Eyes_(1919)_-_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Lobby card from <i>Open Your Eyes</i> (1919)</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BeautDamned1922LobbyCard1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="791" data-file-width="989" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/BeautDamned1922LobbyCard1.jpg/220px-BeautDamned1922LobbyCard1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/BeautDamned1922LobbyCard1.jpg/330px-BeautDamned1922LobbyCard1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/BeautDamned1922LobbyCard1.jpg/440px-BeautDamned1922LobbyCard1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BeautDamned1922LobbyCard1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Lobby card from <i><a href="/wiki/The_Beautiful_and_Damned_(film)" title="The Beautiful and Damned (film)">The Beautiful and Damned</a></i> (1922)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first important deal was the acquisition of the rights to <a href="/wiki/Avery_Hopwood" title="Avery Hopwood">Avery Hopwood</a>'s 1919 Broadway play, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Gold_Diggers_(play)" title="The Gold Diggers (play)">The Gold Diggers</a></i>, from theatrical impresario <a href="/wiki/David_Belasco" title="David Belasco">David Belasco</a>. However, <a href="/wiki/Rin_Tin_Tin" title="Rin Tin Tin">Rin Tin Tin</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN000_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN000-14">[14]</a></sup> a dog brought from France after World War I by an American soldier, established their reputation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN80_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN80-15">[15]</a></sup> Rin Tin Tin debuted in the feature <i><a href="/wiki/Where_the_North_Begins" title="Where the North Begins">Where the North Begins</a></i>. The movie was so successful that Jack signed the dog to star in more films for $1,000 per week.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN000_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN000-14">[14]</a></sup> Rin Tin Tin became the studio's top star.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN000_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN000-14">[14]</a></sup> Jack nicknamed him "The Mortgage Lifter"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN000_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN000-14">[14]</a></sup> and the success boosted <a href="/wiki/Darryl_F._Zanuck" title="Darryl F. Zanuck">Darryl F. Zanuck</a>'s career.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN82_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN82-16">[16]</a></sup> Zanuck eventually became a top producer<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN0976666_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN0976666-17">[17]</a></sup> and between 1928 and 1933 served as Jack's right-hand man and <a href="/wiki/Executive_producer" title="Executive producer">executive producer</a>, with responsibilities including day-to-day film production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-behlmerxii_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-behlmerxii-18">[18]</a></sup> More success came after <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Lubitsch" title="Ernst Lubitsch">Ernst Lubitsch</a> was hired as head director;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN82_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN82-16">[16]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Harry_Rapf" title="Harry Rapf">Harry Rapf</a> left the studio to join <a href="/wiki/Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer" title="Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer">Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas4647_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas4647-19">[19]</a></sup> Lubitsch's film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Marriage_Circle" title="The Marriage Circle">The Marriage Circle</a></i> was the studio's most successful film of 1924, and was on <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> best list for that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN82_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN82-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite the success of Rin Tin Tin and Lubitsch, Warner's remained a lesser studio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN876_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN876-20">[20]</a></sup> Sam and Jack decided to offer Broadway actor <a href="/wiki/John_Barrymore" title="John Barrymore">John Barrymore</a> the lead role in <i><a href="/wiki/Beau_Brummel_(1924_film)" title="Beau Brummel (1924 film)">Beau Brummel</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN876_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN876-20">[20]</a></sup> The film was so successful that Harry signed Barrymore to a long-term contract;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN87680_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN87680-21">[21]</a></sup> like <i><a href="/wiki/The_Marriage_Circle" title="The Marriage Circle">The Marriage Circle</a></i>, <i>Beau Brummell</i> was named one of the ten best films of the year by the <i>Times</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN87680_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN87680-21">[21]</a></sup> By the end of 1924, Warner Bros. was arguably <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_the_United_States" title="Cinema of the United States">Hollywood's</a> most successful independent studio,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN87680_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN87680-21">[21]</a></sup> where it competed with "The Big Three" Studios (<a href="/wiki/First_National_Pictures" title="First National Pictures">First National</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paramount_Pictures" title="Paramount Pictures">Paramount Pictures</a>, and MGM).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-.2BNHAR.28.2A_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-.2BNHAR.28.2A-22">[22]</a></sup> As a result, Harry Warner—while speaking at a convention of 1,500 independent exhibitors in Milwaukee, Wisconsin—was able to convince the filmmakers to spend $500,000 in newspaper advertising,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN99998_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN99998-23">[23]</a></sup> and Harry saw this as an opportunity to establish theaters in cities such as New York and Los Angeles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN99998_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN99998-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>As the studio prospered, it gained backing from <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street" title="Wall Street">Wall Street</a>, and in 1924 <a href="/wiki/Goldman_Sachs" title="Goldman Sachs">Goldman Sachs</a> arranged a major loan. With this new money, the Warners bought the pioneer <a href="/wiki/Vitagraph_Studios" title="Vitagraph Studios">Vitagraph Company</a> which had a nationwide distribution system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN99998_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN99998-23">[23]</a></sup> In 1925, Warners' also experimented in radio, establishing a successful radio station, <a href="/wiki/KFWB" title="KFWB">KFWB</a>, in Los Angeles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN998_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN998-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1925.E2.80.931935:_Sound.2C_color.2C_style">1925–1935: Sound, color, style</span></h3>
<p>Warner Bros. was a pioneer of films with <a href="/wiki/Synchronization" title="Synchronization">synchronized sound</a> (then known as <a href="/wiki/Sound_film" title="Sound film">"talking pictures"</a> or "talkies"). In 1925, at Sam's urging, Warner's agreed to add this feature to their productions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN95_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN95-25">[25]</a></sup> By February 1926, the studio reported a net loss of $333,413.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freedland27_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Freedland27-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:First-nighters_posing_for_the_camera_outside_the_Warners%27_Theater_before_the_premiere_of_%22Don_Juan%22_with_John_Barrymore,_-_NARA_-_535750.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2281" data-file-width="3000" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/First-nighters_posing_for_the_camera_outside_the_Warners%27_Theater_before_the_premiere_of_%22Don_Juan%22_with_John_Barrymore%2C_-_NARA_-_535750.jpg/220px-First-nighters_posing_for_the_camera_outside_the_Warners%27_Theater_before_the_premiere_of_%22Don_Juan%22_with_John_Barrymore%2C_-_NARA_-_535750.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/First-nighters_posing_for_the_camera_outside_the_Warners%27_Theater_before_the_premiere_of_%22Don_Juan%22_with_John_Barrymore%2C_-_NARA_-_535750.jpg/330px-First-nighters_posing_for_the_camera_outside_the_Warners%27_Theater_before_the_premiere_of_%22Don_Juan%22_with_John_Barrymore%2C_-_NARA_-_535750.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/First-nighters_posing_for_the_camera_outside_the_Warners%27_Theater_before_the_premiere_of_%22Don_Juan%22_with_John_Barrymore%2C_-_NARA_-_535750.jpg/440px-First-nighters_posing_for_the_camera_outside_the_Warners%27_Theater_before_the_premiere_of_%22Don_Juan%22_with_John_Barrymore%2C_-_NARA_-_535750.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:First-nighters_posing_for_the_camera_outside_the_Warners%27_Theater_before_the_premiere_of_%22Don_Juan%22_with_John_Barrymore,_-_NARA_-_535750.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Don Juan</i> opens Warners' Theatre</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After a long period denying Sam's request for sound, Harry agreed to change, as long as the studio's use of synchronized sound was for background music purposes only.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN95_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN95-25">[25]</a></sup> The Warners signed a contract with the sound engineer company <a href="/wiki/Western_Electric" title="Western Electric">Western Electric</a> and established <a href="/wiki/Vitaphone" title="Vitaphone">Vitaphone</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN96_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN96-27">[27]</a></sup> In 1926, Vitaphone began making films with music and effects tracks, most notably, in the feature <i><a href="/wiki/Don_Juan_(1926_film)" title="Don Juan (1926 film)">Don Juan</a></i> starring <a href="/wiki/John_Barrymore" title="John Barrymore">John Barrymore</a>. The film was silent, but it featured a large number of Vitaphone shorts at the beginning. To hype <i>Don Juan'</i>s release, Harry acquired the large Piccadilly Theater in <a href="/wiki/Manhattan" title="Manhattan">Manhattan</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, and renamed it Warners' Theatre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas56_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas56-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Don Juan</i> premiered at the Warners' Theatre in New York on August 6, 1926.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas56_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-thomas56-28">[28]</a></sup> Throughout the early history of film distribution, theater owners hired orchestras to attend film showings, where they provided <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soundtracks" title="Soundtracks">soundtracks</a>. Through Vitaphone, Warner Bros. produced eight shorts (which aired at the beginning of every showing of <i>Don Juan</i> across the country) in 1926. Many film production companies questioned the necessity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas57_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas57-29">[29]</a></sup> <i>Don Juan</i> did not recoup its production cost<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN113_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN113-30">[30]</a></sup> and Lubitsch left for MGM.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN876_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN876-20">[20]</a></sup> By April 1927, the Big Five studios (First National, Paramount, MGM, <a href="/wiki/Universal_Studios" title="Universal Studios">Universal</a>, and Producers Distributing) had ruined Warner's,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas59_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas59-31">[31]</a></sup> and Western Electric renewed Warner's Vitaphone contract with terms that allowed other film companies to test sound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas59_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-thomas59-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>As a result of their financial problems, Warner Bros. took the next step and released <i><a href="/wiki/The_Jazz_Singer" title="The Jazz Singer">The Jazz Singer</a></i> starring <a href="/wiki/Al_Jolson" title="Al Jolson">Al Jolson</a>. This movie, which has very little sound dialogue but includes sound segments of Jolson singing, was a sensation. It signaled the beginning of the era of "talking pictures" and the twilight of the <a href="/wiki/Silent_film" title="Silent film">silent</a> era. However, Sam died the night before the opening, preventing the brothers from attending the premiere. Jack became sole head of production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Warner180-181_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Warner180-181-32">[32]</a></sup> Sam's death also had a great effect on Jack's emotional state,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jewishmag.com_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-jewishmag.com-33">[33]</a></sup> as Sam was arguably Jack's inspiration and favorite brother.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas62_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas62-34">[34]</a></sup> In the years to come, Jack kept the studio under tight control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jewishmag.com_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-jewishmag.com-33">[33]</a></sup> Firing employees was common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas100101_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas100101-35">[35]</a></sup> Among those whom Jack fired were Rin Tin Tin (in 1929) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Douglas_Fairbanks,_Jr." title="Douglas Fairbanks, Jr.">Douglas Fairbanks, Jr.</a>—who had served as First National's top star since the brothers acquired the studio in 1928—in 1933.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas100101_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-thomas100101-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>Thanks to the success of <i>The Jazz Singer</i>, the studio was cash-rich. Jolson's next film for the company, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Singing_Fool" title="The Singing Fool">The Singing Fool</a></i> was also a success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN114109_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN114109-36">[36]</a></sup> With the success of these <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_early_Warner_Bros._talking_features" title="List of early Warner Bros. talking features">first talkies</a> (<i>The Jazz Singer</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Lights_of_New_York_(1928_film)" title="Lights of New York (1928 film)">Lights of New York</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Singing_Fool" title="The Singing Fool">The Singing Fool</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Terror_(1928_film)" title="The Terror (1928 film)">The Terror</a></i>), Warner Bros. became a top studio and the brothers were now able to move out from the <a href="/wiki/Poverty_Row" title="Poverty Row">Poverty Row</a> section of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hollywood,_Los_Angeles" title="Hollywood, Los Angeles">Hollywood, Los Angeles</a>, and acquire a <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros._Studios,_Burbank" title="Warner Bros. Studios, Burbank">much larger studio lot</a> in <a href="/wiki/Burbank,_California" title="Burbank, California">Burbank</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-p142_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-p142-37">[37]</a></sup> They expanded by acquiring the Stanley Corporation, a major theater chain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN1146_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN1146-38">[38]</a></sup> This gave them a share in rival <a href="/wiki/First_National_Pictures" title="First National Pictures">First National Pictures</a>, of which Stanley owned one-third.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas65_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas65-39">[39]</a></sup> In a bidding war with <a href="/wiki/William_Fox_(producer)" title="William Fox (producer)">William Fox</a>, Warner Bros. bought more First National shares on September 13, 1928;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN11476_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN11476-40">[40]</a></sup> Jack also appointed Zanuck as the manager of First National Pictures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN11476_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN11476-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Warner_Bros_1920.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="589" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Warner_Bros_1920.jpg/220px-Warner_Bros_1920.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Warner_Bros_1920.jpg/330px-Warner_Bros_1920.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Warner_Bros_1920.jpg/440px-Warner_Bros_1920.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Warner_Bros_1920.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Warner Bros.–First National Studios, Burbank, c. 1928.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1928, Warner Bros. released <i><a href="/wiki/Lights_of_New_York_(1928_film)" title="Lights of New York (1928 film)">Lights of New York</a></i>, the first all-talking feature. Due to its success, the movie industry converted entirely to sound almost overnight. By the end of 1929, all the major studios were exclusively making sound films. In 1929, First National Pictures released their first film with Warner Bros., <i><a href="/wiki/Noah%27s_Ark_(1928_film)" title="Noah's Ark (1928 film)">Noah's Ark</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN114189_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN114189-41">[41]</a></sup> Despite its expensive budget, <i>Noah's Ark</i> was profitable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN114551_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN114551-42">[42]</a></sup> In 1929, Warner Bros. released <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/On_with_the_Show_(1929_film)" title="On with the Show (1929 film)">On with the Show</a></i>, the first all-color all-talking feature. This was followed by <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gold_Diggers_of_Broadway_(film)" title="Gold Diggers of Broadway (film)">Gold Diggers of Broadway</a></i> which was so popular it played in theatres until 1939. The success of these two color pictures caused a color revolution (just as the first all-talkie had created one for talkies). Warner Bros. color films from 1929 to 1931 included <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Show_of_Shows_(film)" title="The Show of Shows (film)">The Show of Shows</a></i> (1929), <i><a href="/wiki/Sally_(1929_film)" title="Sally (1929 film)">Sally</a></i> (1929), <i><a href="/wiki/Bright_Lights_(1930_film)" title="Bright Lights (1930 film)">Bright Lights</a></i> (1930), <i><a href="/wiki/Golden_Dawn_(film)" title="Golden Dawn (film)">Golden Dawn</a></i> (1930), <i><a href="/wiki/Hold_Everything_(1930_film)" title="Hold Everything (1930 film)">Hold Everything</a></i> (1930), <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Song_of_the_Flame_(film)" title="Song of the Flame (film)">Song of the Flame</a></i> (1930), <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Song_of_the_West_(film)" title="Song of the West (film)">Song of the West</a></i> (1930), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Life_of_the_Party_(1930_film)" title="The Life of the Party (1930 film)">The Life of the Party</a></i> (1930), <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sweet_Kitty_Bellairs_(film)" title="Sweet Kitty Bellairs (film)">Sweet Kitty Bellairs</a></i> (1930), <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Under_a_Texas_Moon_(film)" title="Under a Texas Moon (film)">Under A Texas Moon</a></i> (1930), <i><a href="/wiki/Bride_of_the_Regiment" title="Bride of the Regiment">Bride of the Regiment</a></i> (1930), <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viennese_Nights_(film)" title="Viennese Nights (film)">Viennese Nights</a></i> (1931), <i><a href="/wiki/Woman_Hungry_(film)" title="Woman Hungry (film)">Woman Hungry</a></i> (1931), <i><a href="/wiki/Kiss_Me_Again_(1931_film)" title="Kiss Me Again (1931 film)">Kiss Me Again</a></i> (1931), <i><a href="/wiki/Fifty_Million_Frenchmen_(film)" title="Fifty Million Frenchmen (film)">Fifty Million Frenchmen</a></i> (1931) and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manhattan_Parade_(film)" title="Manhattan Parade (film)">Manhattan Parade</a></i> (1932). In addition to these, scores of features were released with <a href="/wiki/Technicolor" title="Technicolor">Technicolor</a> sequences, as well as numerous <a href="/wiki/Technicolor_Specials_(Warner_Bros._series)" title="Technicolor Specials (Warner Bros. series)">Technicolor Specials short subjects</a>. The majority of these color films were musicals.</p>
<p>In 1929, Warner Bros. bought the St. Louis-based theater chain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skouras_Brothers" title="Skouras Brothers">Skouras Brothers</a>. Following this take-over, <a href="/wiki/Spyros_Skouras" title="Spyros Skouras">Spyros Skouras</a>, the driving force of the chain, became general manager of the Warner Brothers Theater Circuit in America. He worked successfully in that post for two years and turned its losses into profits. Harry produced an adaptation of a <a href="/wiki/Cole_Porter" title="Cole Porter">Cole Porter</a> <a href="/wiki/Musical_theatre" title="Musical theatre">musical</a> titled <i><a href="/wiki/Fifty_Million_Frenchmen" title="Fifty Million Frenchmen">Fifty Million Frenchmen</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sperling148_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-sperling148-43">[43]</a></sup> Through First National, the studio's profit increased substantially.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomasfour_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomasfour-44">[44]</a></sup> After the success of the studio's 1929 First National film <i><a href="/wiki/Noah%27s_Ark_(1928_film)" title="Noah's Ark (1928 film)">Noah's Ark</a></i>, Harry agreed to make <a href="/wiki/Michael_Curtiz" title="Michael Curtiz">Michael Curtiz</a> a major director at the Burbank studio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas127_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas127-45">[45]</a></sup> Mort Blumenstock, a First National screenwriter, became a top writer at the brothers' New York headquarters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas208_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas208-46">[46]</a></sup> In the third quarter, Warner Bros. gained complete control of First National, when Harry purchased the company's remaining one-third share from Fox.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN11476_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN11476-40">[40]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Justice" title="United States Department of Justice">Justice Department</a> agreed to allow the purchase if First National was maintained as a separate company.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas67_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas67-47">[47]</a></sup> When the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> hit, Warner asked for and got permission to merge the two studios. Soon afterward Warner Bros. moved to the First National lot in <a href="/wiki/Burbank,_California" title="Burbank, California">Burbank</a>. Though the companies merged, the Justice Department required Warner to release a few films each year under the First National name until 1938. For thirty years, certain Warner productions were identified (mainly for tax purposes) as 'A Warner Bros.–First National Picture.'</p>
<p>In the latter part of 1929, Jack Warner hired <a href="/wiki/George_Arliss" title="George Arliss">George Arliss</a> to star in <i><a href="/wiki/Disraeli_(1929_film)" title="Disraeli (1929 film)">Disraeli</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas77_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas77-48">[48]</a></sup> which was a success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas77_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-thomas77-48">[48]</a></sup> Arliss won an <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Actor" title="Academy Award for Best Actor">Academy Award for Best Actor</a> and went on to star in nine more movies for the studio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas77_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-thomas77-48">[48]</a></sup> In 1930, Harry acquired more theaters in <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_City,_New_Jersey" title="Atlantic City, New Jersey">Atlantic City</a>, despite the beginning of the Great Depression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> In July 1930, the studio's banker, Motley Flint, was murdered by a disgruntled investor in another company.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas72_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas72-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Harry acquired a string of music publishers to form Warner Bros. Music. In April 1930, Warner Bros. acquired <a href="/wiki/Brunswick_Records" title="Brunswick Records">Brunswick Records</a>. Harry obtained radio companies, foreign sound patents and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lithograph" title="Lithograph">lithograph</a> company.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN11476_40-3"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN11476-40">[40]</a></sup> After establishing Warner Bros. Music, Harry appointed his son, Lewis, to manage the company.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas66_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas66-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>By 1931, the studio began to feel the effects of the Depression as the public could no longer afford the tickets. The studio reportedly lost $8 million, and an additional $14 million the following year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sperling160_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-sperling160-52">[52]</a></sup> In 1931, Warner Bros. Music head Lewis Warner died from an infected <a href="/wiki/Wisdom_tooth" title="Wisdom tooth">wisdom tooth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas72_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-thomas72-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Around that time, Zanuck hired screenwriter <a href="/wiki/Wilson_Mizner" title="Wilson Mizner">Wilson Mizner</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas89-92_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas89-92-53">[53]</a></sup> While at the studio, Mizner had hardly any respect for authority and found it difficult to work with Jack,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas89-92_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-thomas89-92-53">[53]</a></sup> but became an asset.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas89-92_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-thomas89-92-53">[53]</a></sup> As time went by, Warner became more tolerant of Mizner and helped invest in Mizner's <a href="/wiki/Brown_Derby" title="Brown Derby">Brown Derby</a> restaurant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas89-92_53-3"><a href="#cite_note-thomas89-92-53">[53]</a></sup> On April 3, 1933, Mizner died from a heart attack.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas93_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas93-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>By 1932, audiences had grown tired of musicals, and the studio was forced to cut musical numbers from many productions and advertise them as straight comedies. The public had begun to associate musicals with color, and thus studios began to abandon its use. Warner Bros. had a contract with Technicolor to produce two more pictures in that process. As a result, the first horror films in color were produced and released by the studio: <i><a href="/wiki/Doctor_X_(film)" title="Doctor X (film)">Doctor X</a></i> (1932) and <i><a href="/wiki/Mystery_of_the_Wax_Museum" title="Mystery of the Wax Museum">Mystery of the Wax Museum</a></i> (1933). In the latter part of 1931, Harry Warner rented the <a href="/wiki/Teddington_Studios" title="Teddington Studios">Teddington Studios</a> in London, England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas110_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas110-55">[55]</a></sup> The studio focused on making "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quota_quickies" title="Quota quickies">quota quickies</a>" for the domestic British market<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Warren_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Warren-56">[56]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Irving_Asher" title="Irving Asher">Irving Asher</a> was appointed as the studio's head producer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Warren_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Warren-56">[56]</a></sup> In 1934, Harry officially purchased the Teddington Studios.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas110_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-thomas110-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>In February 1933, Warner Bros. produced <i><a href="/wiki/42nd_Street_(film)" title="42nd Street (film)">42nd Street</a></i>, a very successful musical under the direction of Lloyd Bacon. Warner assigned Bacon to "more expensive productions including <i><a href="/wiki/Footlight_Parade" title="Footlight Parade">Footlight Parade</a>,</i> <i><a href="/wiki/Wonder_Bar" title="Wonder Bar">Wonder Bar</a>,</i> <a href="/wiki/Broadway_Gondolier" title="Broadway Gondolier">Broadway Gondolier</a>" (which he also starred in), and <i>Gold Diggers</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN190_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN190-58">[58]</a></sup> that saved the company from bankruptcy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> In the wake of <i>42nd Street</i>'s success, the studio produced profitable musicals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN19459087_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN19459087-60">[60]</a></sup> These starred <a href="/wiki/Ruby_Keeler" title="Ruby Keeler">Ruby Keeler</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dick_Powell" title="Dick Powell">Dick Powell</a> and were mostly directed by <a href="/wiki/Busby_Berkeley" title="Busby Berkeley">Busby Berkeley</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN19459044_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN19459044-61">[61]</a></sup> In 1935, the revival suffered a major blow when Berkeley was arrested after killing three people while driving drunk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas86_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas86-62">[62]</a></sup> By the end of the year, people again tired of Warner Bros. musicals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN19459087_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN19459087-60">[60]</a></sup> and the studio—after the huge profits made by 1935 film <i><a href="/wiki/Captain_Blood_(1935_film)" title="Captain Blood (1935 film)">Captain Blood</a></i>—shifted its focus to <a href="/wiki/Errol_Flynn" title="Errol Flynn">Errol Flynn</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swashbuckler_films" title="Swashbuckler films">swashbucklers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN195612_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN195612-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1930.E2.80.931935:_Pre-code_realistic_period">1930–1935: Pre-code realistic period</span></h3>
<p>With the collapse of the market for musicals, Warner Bros., under <a href="/wiki/Darryl_F._Zanuck" title="Darryl F. Zanuck">Zanuck</a> turned to more socially realistic storylines. For its many films about gangsters;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Warner Bros. soon became known as a "gangster studio".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> The studio's first gangster film, <i><a href="/wiki/Little_Caesar_(film)" title="Little Caesar (film)">Little Caesar</a></i>, was a great box office success<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN1987_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN1987-66">[66]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Edward_G._Robinson" title="Edward G. Robinson">Edward G. Robinson</a> starred in many of the subsequent Warner gangster films.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas77-79_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas77-79-67">[67]</a></sup> The studio's next effort, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Public_Enemy" title="The Public Enemy">The Public Enemy</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN987_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN987-68">[68]</a></sup> made <a href="/wiki/James_Cagney" title="James Cagney">James Cagney</a> arguably the studio's new top star,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas81_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas81-69">[69]</a></sup> and Warner Bros. made more gangster films.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN987_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN987-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:239px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:James_Cagney_and_Joan_Blondell_in_Footlight_Parade_trailer.jpg"><img alt="Cagney with his arm around actress Joan Blondell, who has her eyes closed." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="229" data-file-width="294" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/James_Cagney_and_Joan_Blondell_in_Footlight_Parade_trailer.jpg/237px-James_Cagney_and_Joan_Blondell_in_Footlight_Parade_trailer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2e/James_Cagney_and_Joan_Blondell_in_Footlight_Parade_trailer.jpg 1.5x" width="237"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:James_Cagney_and_Joan_Blondell_in_Footlight_Parade_trailer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/James_Cagney" title="James Cagney">James Cagney</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joan_Blondell" title="Joan Blondell">Joan Blondell</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Footlight_Parade" title="Footlight Parade">Footlight Parade</a></i> (1933)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Another gangster film the studio produced was the critically acclaimed <i><a href="/wiki/I_Am_a_Fugitive_from_a_Chain_Gang" title="I Am a Fugitive from a Chain Gang">I Am a Fugitive from a Chain Gang</a></i>, based on a true story and starring <a href="/wiki/Paul_Muni" title="Paul Muni">Paul Muni</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas83_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas83-70">[70]</a></sup> joining Cagney and Robinson as one the studio's top gangster stars<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN867677_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN867677-71">[71]</a></sup> after appearing in the successful film,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN987_68-2"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN987-68">[68]</a></sup> which convinced audiences to question the American legal system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> By January 1933, the film's protagonist Robert Elliot Burns—still imprisoned in New Jersey—and other <a href="/wiki/Chain_gang" title="Chain gang">chain gang</a> prisoners nationwide appealed and were released.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> In January 1933, Georgia chain gang warden J. Harold Hardy—who was also made into a character in the film—sued the studio for displaying "vicious, untrue and false attacks" against him in the film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> After appearing in the Warner's film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Man_Who_Played_God_(1932_film)" title="The Man Who Played God (1932 film)">The Man Who Played God</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Bette_Davis" title="Bette Davis">Bette Davis</a> became a top star.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas81-82_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas81-82-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1933, relief for the studio came after <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a> became president and began the <a href="/wiki/New_Deal" title="New Deal">New Deal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN161_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN161-76">[76]</a></sup> This economic rebound allowed Warner Bros. to again become profitable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN161_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN161-76">[76]</a></sup> The same year, <a href="/wiki/Darryl_F._Zanuck" title="Darryl F. Zanuck">Zanuck</a> quit. Harry Warner's relationship with Zanuck had become strained after Harry strongly opposed allowing Zanuck's film <i><a href="/wiki/Baby_Face_(film)" title="Baby Face (film)">Baby Face</a></i> to step outside <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hays_Code" title="Hays Code">Hays Code</a> boundaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> The studio reduced his salary as a result of losses from the Great Depression,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-p180_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-p180-78">[78]</a></sup> and Harry refused to restore it as the company recovered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TIMEHAR_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-TIMEHAR-79">[79]</a></sup> Zanuck<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-behlmer12_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-behlmer12-80">[80]</a></sup> established his own company.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TIMEHAR_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-TIMEHAR-79">[79]</a></sup> Harry thereafter raised salaries for studio employees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TIMEHAR_79-2"><a href="#cite_note-TIMEHAR-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1933, Warner was able to link up with newspaper tycoon <a href="/wiki/William_Randolph_Hearst" title="William Randolph Hearst">William Randolph Hearst</a>'s Cosmopolitan Films.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas96_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas96-81">[81]</a></sup> Hearst had previously worked with MGM,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas_1990_95_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas_1990_95-82">[82]</a></sup> but ended the association after a dispute with head producer <a href="/wiki/Irving_Thalberg" title="Irving Thalberg">Irving Thalberg</a> over the treatment of Hearst's longstanding mistress, actress <a href="/wiki/Marion_Davies" title="Marion Davies">Marion Davies</a>, who was struggling for box office success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas9596_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas9596-83">[83]</a></sup> Through his partnership with Hearst, Warner signed Davies to a studio contract.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas96_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-thomas96-81">[81]</a></sup> Hearst's company and Davies' films, however, did not increase the studio's profits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas_1990_95_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas_1990_95-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1934, the studio lost over $2.5 million,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRTWBiokj_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-GRTWBiokj-84">[84]</a></sup> of which $500,000 was the result of a 1934 fire at the Burbank studio, destroying 20 years' worth of early Vitagraph, Warner Bros. and First National films.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRTWBiokj_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-GRTWBiokj-84">[84]</a></sup> The following year, Hearst's film adaption of <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/A_Midsummer_Night%27s_Dream_(1935_film)" title="A Midsummer Night's Dream (1935 film)">A Midsummer Night's Dream</a></i> (1935) failed at the <a href="/wiki/Box_office" title="Box office">box office</a> and the studio's net loss increased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> During this time, Harry and six other movie studio figures were indicted for conspiracy to violate the <a href="/wiki/Sherman_Antitrust_Act" title="Sherman Antitrust Act">Sherman Antitrust Act</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRTWBiokj_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-GRTWBiokj-84">[84]</a></sup> through an attempt to gain a monopoly over St Louis movie theaters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> In 1935, Harry was put on trial;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRTWBiokj_84-3"><a href="#cite_note-GRTWBiokj-84">[84]</a></sup> after a mistrial, Harry sold the company's movie theaters and the case was never reopened.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRTWBiokj_84-4"><a href="#cite_note-GRTWBiokj-84">[84]</a></sup> 1935 also saw the studio make a net profit of $674,158.00.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRTWBiokj_84-5"><a href="#cite_note-GRTWBiokj-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Warner_Brother_Studios_from_The_Petrified_Forest_film_trailer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="535" data-file-width="704" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Warner_Brother_Studios_from_The_Petrified_Forest_film_trailer.jpg/220px-Warner_Brother_Studios_from_The_Petrified_Forest_film_trailer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Warner_Brother_Studios_from_The_Petrified_Forest_film_trailer.jpg/330px-Warner_Brother_Studios_from_The_Petrified_Forest_film_trailer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Warner_Brother_Studios_from_The_Petrified_Forest_film_trailer.jpg/440px-Warner_Brother_Studios_from_The_Petrified_Forest_film_trailer.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Warner_Brother_Studios_from_The_Petrified_Forest_film_trailer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The studio as seen in the trailer for <i><a href="/wiki/The_Petrified_Forest" title="The Petrified Forest">The Petrified Forest</a></i> (1936)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By 1936, contracts of musical and silent stars were not renewed replaced by tough-talking, working-class types who better fit these pictures. <a href="/wiki/Dorothy_Mackaill" title="Dorothy Mackaill">Dorothy Mackaill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dolores_del_R%C3%ADo" title="Dolores del Río">Dolores del Río</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bebe_Daniels" title="Bebe Daniels">Bebe Daniels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frank_Fay_(American_actor)" title="Frank Fay (American actor)">Frank Fay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Winnie_Lightner" title="Winnie Lightner">Winnie Lightner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bernice_Claire" title="Bernice Claire">Bernice Claire</a>, Alexander Gray, <a href="/wiki/Alice_White" title="Alice White">Alice White</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jack_Mulhall" title="Jack Mulhall">Jack Mulhall</a> that had characterized the urban, modern, and sophisticated attitude of the 1920s gave way to <a href="/wiki/James_Cagney" title="James Cagney">James Cagney</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joan_Blondell" title="Joan Blondell">Joan Blondell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edward_G._Robinson" title="Edward G. Robinson">Edward G. Robinson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Warren_William" title="Warren William">Warren William</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barbara_Stanwyck" title="Barbara Stanwyck">Barbara Stanwyck</a>, who would be more acceptable to the common man. The studio was one of the most prolific producers of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-Code" title="Pre-Code">Pre-Code</a> pictures and had a lot of trouble with the censors once they started clamping down on what they considered indecency (around 1934).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN181_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN181-87">[87]</a></sup> As a result, Warner Bros. turned to historical pictures from around 1935 to avoid confrontations with the Breen office. In 1936, following the success of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Petrified_Forest" title="The Petrified Forest">The Petrified Forest</a></i>, Jack signed <a href="/wiki/Humphrey_Bogart" title="Humphrey Bogart">Humphrey Bogart</a> to a studio contract.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas109_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas109-88">[88]</a></sup> Warner, however, did not think Bogart was star material,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas109110_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas109110-89">[89]</a></sup> and cast Bogart in infrequent roles as a villain opposite either James Cagney or Edward Robinson over the next five years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas109_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-thomas109-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>After <a href="/wiki/Hal_B._Wallis" title="Hal B. Wallis">Hal B. Wallis</a> succeeded Zanuck in 1933,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas88_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas88-90">[90]</a></sup> and the Hays Code began to be enforced in 1935, the studio was forced to abandon this realistic approach in order to produce more moralistic, idealized pictures. The studio's historical dramas, <a href="/wiki/Melodrama" title="Melodrama">melodramas</a> (or "women's pictures"), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swashbuckler_films" title="Swashbuckler films">swashbucklers</a>, and adaptations of best-sellers, with stars like <a href="/wiki/Bette_Davis" title="Bette Davis">Bette Davis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Olivia_de_Havilland" title="Olivia de Havilland">Olivia de Havilland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Muni" title="Paul Muni">Paul Muni</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Errol_Flynn" title="Errol Flynn">Errol Flynn</a> avoided the censors. In 1936, Bette Davis, by now arguably the studio's top star,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBT.25.5E.24HYNA66_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-HBT.25.5E.24HYNA66-91">[91]</a></sup> was unhappy with her roles. She traveled to England and tried to break her contract.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBT.25.5E.24HYNA66_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-HBT.25.5E.24HYNA66-91">[91]</a></sup> Davis lost the lawsuit and returned to America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-.5E.24HYNA66_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-.5E.24HYNA66-92">[92]</a></sup> Although many of the studio's employees had problems with Jack Warner, they considered Albert and Harry fair.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas115_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas115-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Code_era">Code era</span></h3>
<p>In the 1930s many actors and actresses disappeared who had characterized the realistic pre-Code era but who were not suited to the new trend into moral and idealized pictures. Warner Bros. remained a top studio in <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_the_United_States" title="Cinema of the United States">Hollywood</a>, but this changed after 1935 as other studios, notably MGM, quickly overshadowed the prestige and glamor that previously characterized Warner Bros. However, in the late 1930s, Bette Davis became the studio's top draw and was even dubbed as "The Fifth Warner Brother."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Humphrey_Bogart_in_The_Petrified_Forest_film_trailer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="533" data-file-width="703" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Humphrey_Bogart_in_The_Petrified_Forest_film_trailer.jpg/220px-Humphrey_Bogart_in_The_Petrified_Forest_film_trailer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Humphrey_Bogart_in_The_Petrified_Forest_film_trailer.jpg/330px-Humphrey_Bogart_in_The_Petrified_Forest_film_trailer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Humphrey_Bogart_in_The_Petrified_Forest_film_trailer.jpg/440px-Humphrey_Bogart_in_The_Petrified_Forest_film_trailer.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Humphrey_Bogart_in_The_Petrified_Forest_film_trailer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Humphrey_Bogart" title="Humphrey Bogart">Humphrey Bogart</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Petrified_Forest" title="The Petrified Forest">The Petrified Forest</a></i> (1936)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1935, Cagney sued Jack Warner for breach of contract.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas104-106_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas104-106-95">[95]</a></sup> Cagney claimed Warner had forced him to star in more films than his contract required.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas104-106_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-thomas104-106-95">[95]</a></sup> Cagney eventually dropped his lawsuit after a cash settlement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas105_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas105-96">[96]</a></sup> Nevertheless, Cagney left the studio to establish an independent film company with his brother Bill.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas106_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas106-97">[97]</a></sup> The Cagneys released their films though <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grand_National_Films" title="Grand National Films">Grand National Films</a>, however they were not able to get good financing<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas106_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-thomas106-97">[97]</a></sup> and ran out of money after their third film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas106_97-2"><a href="#cite_note-thomas106-97">[97]</a></sup> Cagney then agreed to return to Warner Bros., after Jack agreed to a contract guaranteeing Cagney would be treated to his own terms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas106_97-3"><a href="#cite_note-thomas106-97">[97]</a></sup> After the success of <i><a href="/wiki/Yankee_Doodle_Dandy" title="Yankee Doodle Dandy">Yankee Doodle Dandy</a></i> at the box office, Cagney again questioned if the studio would meet his salary demand<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas144z_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas144z-98">[98]</a></sup> and again quit to form his own film production and distribution company with Bill.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas144z_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas144z-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another employee with whom Warner had troubles was studio producer <a href="/wiki/Bryan_Foy" title="Bryan Foy">Bryan Foy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas116_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas116-99">[99]</a></sup> In 1936, Wallis hired Foy as a producer for the studio's low budget <a href="/wiki/B_movie" title="B movie">B-films</a> leading to his nickname "the keeper of the B's".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas115_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-thomas115-93">[93]</a></sup> Foy was able to garnish arguably more profits than any other B-film producer at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas115_93-2"><a href="#cite_note-thomas115-93">[93]</a></sup> During Foy's time at the studio, however, Warner fired him seven different times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas116_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-thomas116-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>During 1936, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Story_of_Louis_Pasteur" title="The Story of Louis Pasteur">The Story of Louis Pasteur</a></i> proved a box office success<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas114_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas114-100">[100]</a></sup> and star Paul Muni won the Oscar for Best Actor in March 1937.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas114_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-thomas114-100">[100]</a></sup> The studio's 1937 film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Life_of_Emile_Zola" title="The Life of Emile Zola">The Life of Emile Zola</a></i> gave the studio its first Best Picture Oscar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas114_100-2"><a href="#cite_note-thomas114-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1937, the studio hired Midwestern radio announcer <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a>. Although Reagan was initially a B-film actor, Warner Bros. was impressed by his performance in the final scene of <i><a href="/wiki/Knute_Rockne,_All_American" title="Knute Rockne, All American">Knute Rockne, All American</a></i>, and agreed to pair him with Flynn in <i><a href="/wiki/Santa_Fe_Trail_(film)" title="Santa Fe Trail (film)">Santa Fe Trail</a></i> (1940). Reagan then returned to B-films.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas117_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas117-101">[101]</a></sup> After his performance in the studio's 1942 <i><a href="/wiki/Kings_Row" title="Kings Row">Kings Row</a></i>, Warner decided to make Reagan a top star and signed him to a new contract, tripling his salary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas117118_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas117118-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1936, Harry's daughter Doris read a copy of Margaret Mitchell's <i><a href="/wiki/Gone_with_the_Wind_(novel)" title="Gone with the Wind (novel)">Gone with the Wind</a></i> and was interested in making a film adaptation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sperling220_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-sperling220-103">[103]</a></sup> Doris offered Mitchell $50,000 for screen rights. Jack vetoed the deal, realizing it would be an expensive production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sperling220_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-sperling220-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>Major <a href="/wiki/Paramount_Pictures" title="Paramount Pictures">Paramount</a> star <a href="/wiki/George_Raft" title="George Raft">George Raft</a> also eventually proved to be a problem for Jack.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas123125_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas123125-104">[104]</a></sup> Warner had signed him in 1939, finally bringing the third top 1930s gangster actor into the Warners fold, knowing that he could carry any gangster picture when either Robinson or Cagney were on suspension.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas123125_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-thomas123125-104">[104]</a></sup> Raft had difficulty working with Bogart and refused to co-star with him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas124_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas124-105">[105]</a></sup> Eventually, Warner agreed to release Raft from his contract in 1943.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas125_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas125-106">[106]</a></sup> After Raft had turned the role down, the studio gave Bogart the role of "Mad Dog" Roy Earle in the 1941 film <i><a href="/wiki/High_Sierra_(film)" title="High Sierra (film)">High Sierra</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas125_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-thomas125-106">[106]</a></sup> which helped establish him as a top star.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas125126_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas125126-107">[107]</a></sup> Following <i>High Sierra</i> and after Raft had once again turned the part down, Bogart was given the leading role in <a href="/wiki/John_Huston" title="John Huston">John Huston</a>'s successful <a href="/wiki/The_Maltese_Falcon_(1941_film)" title="The Maltese Falcon (1941 film)">1941 remake</a> of the studio's 1931 pre-Code film, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Maltese_Falcon_(1931_film)" title="The Maltese Falcon (1931 film)">The Maltese Falcon</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas126127_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas126127-108">[108]</a></sup> based upon the <a href="/wiki/Dashiell_Hammett" title="Dashiell Hammett">Dashiell Hammett</a> novel.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Warner.27s_cartoons">Warner's cartoons</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros._Cartoons" title="Warner Bros. Cartoons">Warner Bros. Cartoons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros._Animation" title="Warner Bros. Animation">Warner Bros. Animation</a></div>
<p>Warner's <a href="/wiki/Cartoon" title="Cartoon">cartoon</a> unit had its roots in the independent <a href="/wiki/Harman_and_Ising" title="Harman and Ising">Harman and Ising</a> studio. From 1930 to 1933, <a href="/wiki/The_Walt_Disney_Company" title="The Walt Disney Company">Disney</a> alumni Hugh Harman and Rudolf Ising produced musical cartoons for <a href="/wiki/Leon_Schlesinger" title="Leon Schlesinger">Leon Schlesinger</a>, who sold them to Warner. Harman and Ising introduced their character <a href="/wiki/Bosko" title="Bosko">Bosko</a> in the first <i><a href="/wiki/Looney_Tunes" title="Looney Tunes">Looney Tunes</a></i> cartoon, <i><a href="/wiki/Sinkin%27_in_the_Bathtub" title="Sinkin' in the Bathtub">Sinkin' in the Bathtub</a></i>, and created a sister series, <i><a href="/wiki/Merrie_Melodies" title="Merrie Melodies">Merrie Melodies</a></i>, in 1931.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN987980_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN987980-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>Harman and Ising broke away from Schlesinger in 1933 due to a contractual dispute, taking Bosko with them to MGM. As a result, Schlesinger started his own studio, <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros._Cartoons" title="Warner Bros. Cartoons">Leon Schlesinger Productions</a>, which continued with <i>Merrie Melodies</i> while starting production on <i>Looney Tunes</i> starring <a href="/wiki/Buddy_(Looney_Tunes)" title="Buddy (Looney Tunes)">Buddy</a>, a Bosko clone. By the end of World War 2, a new Schlesinger production team, including directors <a href="/wiki/Friz_Freleng" title="Friz Freleng">Friz Freleng</a> (started in 1934), <a href="/wiki/Tex_Avery" title="Tex Avery">Tex Avery</a> (started in 1935), <a href="/wiki/Frank_Tashlin" title="Frank Tashlin">Frank Tashlin</a> (started in 1936), <a href="/wiki/Bob_Clampett" title="Bob Clampett">Bob Clampett</a> (started in 1937), <a href="/wiki/Chuck_Jones" title="Chuck Jones">Chuck Jones</a> (started in 1938), and <a href="/wiki/Robert_McKimson" title="Robert McKimson">Bob McKimson</a> (started in 1945), was formed. Schlesinger's staff developed a fast-paced, irreverent style that made their cartoons globally popular.</p>
<p>In 1935, Avery directed cartoons starring <a href="/wiki/Porky_Pig" title="Porky Pig">Porky Pig</a>, which established the character as the studio's first animated star.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TermiteTerrace_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-TermiteTerrace-110">[110]</a></sup> In addition to Porky, Warner Bros. cartoon characters <a href="/wiki/Daffy_Duck" title="Daffy Duck">Daffy Duck</a> (who debuted in the 1937 short <i><a href="/wiki/Porky%27s_Duck_Hunt" title="Porky's Duck Hunt">Porky's Duck Hunt</a></i>), <a href="/wiki/Elmer_Fudd" title="Elmer Fudd">Elmer Fudd</a> (who debuted in the 1940 short <i><a href="/wiki/Elmer%27s_Candid_Camera" title="Elmer's Candid Camera">Elmer's Candid Camera</a></i>), <a href="/wiki/Bugs_Bunny" title="Bugs Bunny">Bugs Bunny</a> (who debuted in the 1940 short <i><a href="/wiki/A_Wild_Hare" title="A Wild Hare">A Wild Hare</a></i><a href="/wiki/A_Wild_Hare" title="A Wild Hare">)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tweety" title="Tweety">Tweety</a> (who debuted in the 1942 short <i><a href="/wiki/A_Tale_of_Two_Kitties" title="A Tale of Two Kitties">A Tale of Two Kitties</a></i>) acheived star power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> By 1942, the Schlesinger studio had surpassed Walt Disney Studios as the most successful producer of animated shorts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>Warner Bros. eventually bought Schlesinger's cartoon unit in 1944 and renamed it <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros._Cartoons" title="Warner Bros. Cartoons">Warner Bros. Cartoons</a>. Unfortunately, the unit was indifferently treated by senior management, beginning with the installation of <a href="/wiki/Edward_Selzer" title="Edward Selzer">Edward Selzer</a> as senior producer, whom the creative staff considered an interfering incompetent. Jack had little regard for the company's short film product and reputedly was so ignorant about the animation division of the studio that he was mistakenly convinced that the unit produced cartoons of <a href="/wiki/Mickey_Mouse" title="Mickey Mouse">Mickey Mouse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Animation_Studios" title="Walt Disney Animation Studios">Walt Disney Productions</a>' flagship character.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas212_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas212-113">[113]</a></sup> He sold off the unit's pre-August 1948 library for $3,000 each, which proved a shortsighted transaction in light of its eventual value.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas212_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-thomas212-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p>Warner Bros. Cartoons continued, with intermittent interruptions, until 1969 when it was dissolved as the parent company ceased film shorts entirely. Characters such as Bugs Bunny, Daffy Duck, <a href="/wiki/Tweety" title="Tweety">Tweety Bird</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sylvester_(Looney_Tunes)" title="Sylvester (Looney Tunes)">Sylvester</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Porky_pig" title="Porky pig">Porky Pig</a> became central to the company's image in subsequent decades. Bugs in particular remains a mascot to Warner Bros., its various divisions and <a href="/wiki/Six_Flags" title="Six Flags">Six Flags</a> (which Time Warner once owned). The success of the compilation film, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Bugs_Bunny/Road_Runner_Movie" title="The Bugs Bunny/Road Runner Movie">The Bugs Bunny/Road Runner Movie</a></i> in 1980, featuring the archived film of these characters prompted Warner Brothers to organize <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros._Animation" title="Warner Bros. Animation">Warner Bros. Animation</a> as a new production division to restart production of original material.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II">World War II</span></h3>
<p>According to Warner's autobiography, prior to US entry in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, Philip Kauffman, Warner Bros. German sales head, was murdered by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nazis" title="Nazis">Nazis</a> in Berlin in 1936.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> Harry produced the successful anti-German film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Life_of_Emile_Zola" title="The Life of Emile Zola">The Life of Emile Zola</a></i> (1937).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRTWBiok5j1y.29_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-GRTWBiok5j1y.29-117">[117]</a></sup> After that, Harry supervised the production of more anti-German films, including <i><a href="/wiki/Confessions_of_a_Nazi_Spy" title="Confessions of a Nazi Spy">Confessions of a Nazi Spy</a></i> (1939),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRTWBiokj1y233.29_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-GRTWBiokj1y233.29-118">[118]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sea_Hawk_(1940_film)" title="The Sea Hawk (1940 film)">The Sea Hawk</a></i> (1940), which made <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_Phillip_II_of_Spain" title="King Phillip II of Spain">King Phillip II</a> an equivalent of <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Hitler</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRTWBiokj1y247.29_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-GRTWBiokj1y247.29-119">[119]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Sergeant_York_(film)" title="Sergeant York (film)">Sergeant York</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRTWBiokj1y2rt.29_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-GRTWBiokj1y2rt.29-120">[120]</a></sup> and <i>You're In The Army Now</i> (1941).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRTWBiokj1y2rt.29_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-GRTWBiokj1y2rt.29-120">[120]</a></sup> Harry then decided to focus on producing war films.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRTWBiokj1y.29_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-GRTWBiokj1y.29-121">[121]</a></sup> Warners' cut its film production in half during the war, eliminating its B Pictures unit in 1941. Bryan Foy joined Twentieth Century Fox.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:177px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bette_Davis_in_Now_Voyager_trailer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="531" data-file-width="607" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Bette_Davis_in_Now_Voyager_trailer.jpg/175px-Bette_Davis_in_Now_Voyager_trailer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Bette_Davis_in_Now_Voyager_trailer.jpg/263px-Bette_Davis_in_Now_Voyager_trailer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Bette_Davis_in_Now_Voyager_trailer.jpg/350px-Bette_Davis_in_Now_Voyager_trailer.jpg 2x" width="175"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bette_Davis_in_Now_Voyager_trailer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bette_Davis" title="Bette Davis">Bette Davis</a> in <i>Now, Voyager</i> (1942)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the war era, the studio made <i><a href="/wiki/Casablanca_(film)" title="Casablanca (film)">Casablanca</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Now,_Voyager" title="Now, Voyager">Now, Voyager</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Yankee_Doodle_Dandy" title="Yankee Doodle Dandy">Yankee Doodle Dandy</a></i> (all 1942), <i><a href="/wiki/This_Is_the_Army" title="This Is the Army">This Is the Army</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Mission_to_Moscow" title="Mission to Moscow">Mission to Moscow</a></i> (both 1943);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pages247_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-pages247-123">[123]</a></sup> the latter became controversial a few years afterwards. At the premieres of <i>Yankee Doodle Dandy</i> (in Los Angeles, New York, and London), audiences purchased $15.6 million in <a href="/wiki/War_bond" title="War bond">war bonds</a> for the governments of England and the United States. By the middle of 1943, however, audiences had tired of war films, but Warner continued to produce them, losing money. In honor of the studio's contributions to the cause, the Navy named a <a href="/wiki/Liberty_ship" title="Liberty ship">Liberty ship</a> after the brothers' father, Benjamin Warner. Harry christened the ship. By the time the war ended, $20 million in war bonds were purchased through the studio, <a href="/wiki/American_Red_Cross" title="American Red Cross">the Red Cross</a> collected 5,200 pints of <a href="/wiki/Blood_plasma" title="Blood plasma">plasma</a> from studio employees<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pages247_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-pages247-123">[123]</a></sup> and 763 of the studio's employees served in the armed forces, including Harry Warner's son-in-law <a href="/wiki/Milton_Sperling" title="Milton Sperling">Milton Sperling</a> and Jack's son Jack Warner, Jr.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRTWBiokj1y.29_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-GRTWBiokj1y.29-121">[121]</a></sup> Following a dispute over ownership of <i>Casablanca</i>'s Oscar for Best Picture, Wallis resigned. After <i>Casablanca</i> made Bogart a top star, Bogart's relationship with Jack deteriorated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas144z_98-2"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas144z-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1943, <a href="/wiki/Olivia_de_Havilland" title="Olivia de Havilland">Olivia de Havilland</a> (whom Warner was loaning to different studios) sued Warner for breach of contract.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas145_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas145-124">[124]</a></sup> De Haviland had refused to portray famed abolitionist <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Blackwell" title="Elizabeth Blackwell">Elizabeth Blackwell</a> in an upcoming film for Columbia Pictures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas145_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas145-124">[124]</a></sup> Warner responded by sending 150 telegrams to different film production companies, warning them not to hire her for any role.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas145_124-2"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas145-124">[124]</a></sup> Afterwards, de Haviland discovered employment contracts in the United States could only last seven years; de Haviland had been under contract with the studio since 1935.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas98_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas98-125">[125]</a></sup> The court ruled in de Haviland's favor<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas145_124-3"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas145-124">[124]</a></sup> and she left the studio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas145_124-4"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas145-124">[124]</a></sup> Through de Haviland's victory, many of the studio's longtime actors were now freed from their contracts,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas145_124-5"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas145-124">[124]</a></sup> and Harry decided to terminate the studio's suspension policy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas148_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas148-126">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>The same year, Jack signed newly released MGM actress <a href="/wiki/Joan_Crawford" title="Joan Crawford">Joan Crawford</a>, a former top star who found her career fading.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas150_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas150-127">[127]</a></sup> Crawford's first role with the studio was 1944's <i><a href="/wiki/Hollywood_Canteen_(film)" title="Hollywood Canteen (film)">Hollywood Canteen</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas151_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas151-128">[128]</a></sup> Her first starring role at the studio, in the title role as <i><a href="/wiki/Mildred_Pierce_(film)" title="Mildred Pierce (film)">Mildred Pierce</a></i> (1945), revived her career<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas151_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas151-128">[128]</a></sup> and earned her an Oscar for Best Actress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas152_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas152-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="After_World_War_II:_changing_hands">After World War II: changing hands</span></h3>
<p>In the post-war years, Warner Bros. continued to create new stars, including <a href="/wiki/Lauren_Bacall" title="Lauren Bacall">Lauren Bacall</a> and <a href="/wiki/Doris_Day" title="Doris Day">Doris Day</a>. The studio prospered greatly after the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LUI_.28_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-LUI_.28-130">[130]</a></sup> By 1946, company payroll reached $600,000 a week<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LUI_.28_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-LUI_.28-130">[130]</a></sup> and net profit topped $19.4 million.</p>
<p>Jack Warner continued to refuse to meet <a href="/wiki/Screen_Actors_Guild" title="Screen Actors Guild">Screen Actors Guild</a> salary demands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas163_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas163-131">[131]</a></sup> In September 1946, employees engaged in a month-long strike.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas163_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-thomas163-131">[131]</a></sup> In retaliation, Warner—during his 1947 testimony before <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">Congress</a> about <i>Mission to Moscow</i>—accused multiple employees of ties to Communists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas164_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas164-132">[132]</a></sup> By the end of 1947, the studio reached a record net profit of $22 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRTWBiodry2prt.29_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-GRTWBiodry2prt.29-133">[133]</a></sup></p>
<p>On January 5, 1948, Warner offered the first color <a href="/wiki/Newsreel" title="Newsreel">newsreel</a>, covering the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tournament_of_Roses_Parade" title="Tournament of Roses Parade">Tournament of Roses Parade</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Rose_Bowl_Game" title="Rose Bowl Game">Rose Bowl Game</a>. In 1948, Bette Davis, still their top actress and now hostile to Jack, was a big problem for Harry after she and others left the studio after completing the film <i><a href="/wiki/Beyond_the_Forest" title="Beyond the Forest">Beyond the Forest</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas175176_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas175176-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p>Warner was a party to the <i><a href="/wiki/United_States_v._Paramount_Pictures,_Inc." title="United States v. Paramount Pictures, Inc.">United States v. Paramount Pictures, Inc.</a></i> anti-trust case of the 1940s. This action, brought by the Justice Department and the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Trade_Commission" title="Federal Trade Commission">Federal Trade Commission</a>, claimed the five integrated studio-theater chain combinations restrained competition. The <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court</a> heard the case in 1948, and ruled for the government. As a result, Warner and four other major studios were forced to separate production from exhibition. In 1949, the studio's net profit was only $10 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRTWBiodry2prt.29_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-GRTWBiodry2prt.29-133">[133]</a></sup></p>
<p>Warner Bros. had two semi-independent production companies that released films through the studio.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> One of these was <a href="/wiki/Milton_Sperling" title="Milton Sperling">Sperling</a>'s <a href="/wiki/United_States_Pictures" title="United States Pictures">United States Pictures</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vivien_Leigh_in_Streetcar_Named_Desire_trailer_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="538" data-file-width="701" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Vivien_Leigh_in_Streetcar_Named_Desire_trailer_2.jpg/220px-Vivien_Leigh_in_Streetcar_Named_Desire_trailer_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Vivien_Leigh_in_Streetcar_Named_Desire_trailer_2.jpg/330px-Vivien_Leigh_in_Streetcar_Named_Desire_trailer_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Vivien_Leigh_in_Streetcar_Named_Desire_trailer_2.jpg/440px-Vivien_Leigh_in_Streetcar_Named_Desire_trailer_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vivien_Leigh_in_Streetcar_Named_Desire_trailer_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Vivien_Leigh" title="Vivien Leigh">Vivien Leigh</a> as <a href="/wiki/Blanche_DuBois" title="Blanche DuBois">Blanche DuBois</a> in <a href="/wiki/A_Streetcar_Named_Desire_(1951_film)" title="A Streetcar Named Desire (1951 film)"><i>A Streetcar Named Desire</i> (1951)</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the early 1950s, the threat of television emerged.
