In 1953, Jack decided to copy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268lk_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-268lk-136">[136]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/United_Artists" title="United Artists">United Artists</a> successful 3D film <i><a href="/wiki/Bwana_Devil" title="Bwana Devil">Bwana Devil</a></i>, releasing his own 3D films beginning with <i><a href="/wiki/House_of_Wax_(1953_film)" title="House of Wax (1953 film)">House of Wax</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-p287_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-p287-137">[137]</a></sup> However, 3D films soon lost their appeal among moviegoers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas191_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas191-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<p>3D almost caused the demise of the Warner Bros. cartoon studio. Having completed a 3D Bugs Bunny cartoon, <i><a href="/wiki/Lumber_Jack-Rabbit" title="Lumber Jack-Rabbit">Lumber Jack-Rabbit</a></i>, Jack Warner ordered the animation unit to be shut down, erroneously believing that all cartoons hence would be produced in the 3D process. Several months later, Warner relented and reopened the cartoon studio. Fortunately, Warner Bros. had enough of a backlog of cartoons and a healthy reissue program so that there was no noticeable interruption in the release schedule.</p>
<p>In 1952, Warner Bros. made their first film (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carson_City_(1952_film)" title="Carson City (1952 film)">Carson City</a></i>) in "Warnercolor", the studio's name for <a href="/wiki/Eastmancolor" title="Eastmancolor">Eastmancolor</a>.</p>
<p>After the downfall of 3D films, Harry Warner decided to use <a href="/wiki/CinemaScope" title="CinemaScope">CinemaScope</a> in future Warner Bros. films.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-W2878_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-W2878-139">[139]</a></sup> One of the studio's first <a href="/wiki/CinemaScope" title="CinemaScope">CinemaScope</a> films, <i><a href="/wiki/The_High_and_the_Mighty_(film)" title="The High and the Mighty (film)">The High and the Mighty</a></i> (owned by <a href="/wiki/John_Wayne" title="John Wayne">John Wayne</a>'s company <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Batjac" title="Batjac">Batjac</a>), enabled the studio to show a profit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-W288_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-W288-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
<p>Early in 1953, Warner's theater holdings were spun off as Stanley Warner Theaters; Stanley Warner's non-theater holdings were sold to Simon Fabian Enterprises,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Purch56_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Purch56-141">[141]</a></sup> and its theaters merged with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RKO" title="RKO">RKO</a> Theatres to become RKO-Stanley Warner Theatres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<p>By 1956, the studio was losing money,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN82009787_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN82009787-143">[143]</a></sup> declining from 1953's net profit of $2.9 million<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas190_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas190-144">[144]</a></sup> and the next two years of between $2 and $4 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas225_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas225-145">[145]</a></sup> In February 13, 1956, Jack Warner sold the rights to all of his pre-1950 films to <a href="/wiki/Associated_Artists_Productions" title="Associated Artists Productions">Associated Artists Productions</a> (which merged with <a href="/wiki/United_Artists_Television" title="United Artists Television">United Artists Television</a> in 1958, and was subsequently acquired by <a href="/wiki/Turner_Broadcasting_System" title="Turner Broadcasting System">Turner Broadcasting System</a> in early 1986 as part of a failed takeover of <a href="/wiki/Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer" title="Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer">MGM/UA</a> by <a href="/wiki/Ted_Turner" title="Ted Turner">Ted Turner</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ymrt_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-ymrt-146">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aap_exceptions_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-aap_exceptions-147">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archive.org_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-archive.org-148">[148]</a></sup></p>
<p>In May 1956, the brothers announced they were putting Warner Bros. on the market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Purch88_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Purch88-149">[149]</a></sup> Jack secretly organized a syndicate—headed by Boston banker Serge Semenenko<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN82009787_143-1"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN82009787-143">[143]</a></sup>– to purchase 90% of the stock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN82009787_143-2"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN82009787-143">[143]</a></sup> After the three brothers sold, Jack—through his under-the-table deal—joined Semenenko's syndicate<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN308_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN308-150">[150]</a></sup> and bought back all his stock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN308_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN308-150">[150]</a></sup> Shortly after the deal was completed in July,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas226_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas226-151">[151]</a></sup> Jack—now the company's largest stockholder—appointed himself its new president.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBTN.3DA89787_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-HBTN.3DA89787-152">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas226_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-thomas226-151">[151]</a></sup> Shortly after the deal closed, Jack announced the company and its subsidiaries would be "directed more vigorously to the acquisition of the most important story properties, talents, and to the production of the finest motion pictures possible."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vindy-07-12-56_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-vindy-07-12-56-153">[153]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Warner_Bros._Television_and_Warner_Bros._Records">Warner Bros. Television and Warner Bros. Records</span></h3>
<p>By 1949, with the success of television threatening the film industry more and more, Harry Warner decided to emphasize television production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268lk_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-268lk-136">[136]</a></sup> However, the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Communications_Commission" title="Federal Communications Commission">Federal Communications Commission</a> (FCC) would not permit it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268lk_136-2"><a href="#cite_note-268lk-136">[136]</a></sup> After an unsuccessful attempt to convince other movie studio bosses to switch, Harry abandoned his television efforts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-p.287_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-p.287-154">[154]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jack had problems with <a href="/wiki/Milton_Berle" title="Milton Berle">Milton Berle</a>'s unsuccessful film <i><a href="/wiki/Always_Leave_Them_Laughing" title="Always Leave Them Laughing">Always Leave Them Laughing</a></i> during the peak of Berle's television popularity. Warner felt that Berle was not strong enough to carry a film and that people would not pay to see the man they could see on television for free. However, Jack was pressured into using Berle, replacing <a href="/wiki/Danny_Kaye" title="Danny Kaye">Danny Kaye</a> with him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> Berle's outrageous behaviour on the set and the film's massive failure led to Jack banning television sets from film sets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:James_Garner_Bret_Maverick_Jack_Kelly_Bart_Maverick.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="897" data-file-width="748" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/James_Garner_Bret_Maverick_Jack_Kelly_Bart_Maverick.JPG/160px-James_Garner_Bret_Maverick_Jack_Kelly_Bart_Maverick.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/James_Garner_Bret_Maverick_Jack_Kelly_Bart_Maverick.JPG/240px-James_Garner_Bret_Maverick_Jack_Kelly_Bart_Maverick.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/James_Garner_Bret_Maverick_Jack_Kelly_Bart_Maverick.JPG/320px-James_Garner_Bret_Maverick_Jack_Kelly_Bart_Maverick.JPG 2x" width="160"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:James_Garner_Bret_Maverick_Jack_Kelly_Bart_Maverick.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/James_Garner" title="James Garner">James Garner</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jack_Kelly_(actor)" title="Jack Kelly (actor)">Jack Kelly</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Maverick_(TV_series)" title="Maverick (TV series)">Maverick</a></i> (1957).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On March 21, 1955, the studio was finally able to engage in television through the successful <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros._Television" title="Warner Bros. Television">Warner Bros. Television</a> unit run by <a href="/wiki/William_T._Orr" title="William T. Orr">William T. Orr</a>, Jack Warner's son-in-law. Warner Bros. Television provided <a href="/wiki/American_Broadcasting_Company" title="American Broadcasting Company">ABC</a> with a weekly show, <i><a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros._Presents" title="Warner Bros. Presents">Warner Bros. Presents</a>.</i> The show featured rotating shows based on three film successes, <i><a href="/wiki/Kings_Row#Television_series" title="Kings Row">Kings Row</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Casablanca_(film)#Sequels_and_other_versions" title="Casablanca (film)">Casablanca</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Cheyenne_(film)" title="Cheyenne (film)">Cheyenne</a></i>, followed by a promotion for a new film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbddpp_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbddpp-157">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas192_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas192-158">[158]</a></sup> It was not a success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas193_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas193-159">[159]</a></sup> The studio's next effort was to make a weekly series out of <i>Cheyenne</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas194_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas194-160">[160]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Cheyenne_(1955_TV_series)" title="Cheyenne (1955 TV series)">Cheyenne</a></i> was television's first hour-long <a href="/wiki/Western_(genre)" title="Western (genre)">Western</a>. Two episodes were placed together for feature film release outside the United States. In the tradition of their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/B_pictures" title="B pictures">B pictures</a>, the studio followed up with a series of rapidly produced popular <a href="/wiki/Western_(genre)" title="Western (genre)">Westerns</a>, such as writer/producer <a href="/wiki/Roy_Huggins" title="Roy Huggins">Roy Huggins</a>' critically lauded <i><a href="/wiki/Maverick_(TV_series)" title="Maverick (TV series)">Maverick</a></i> as well as <i><a href="/wiki/Sugarfoot" title="Sugarfoot">Sugarfoot</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Bronco_(TV_series)" title="Bronco (TV series)">Bronco</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Lawman_(TV_series)" title="Lawman (TV series)">Lawman</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Alaskans" title="The Alaskans">The Alaskans</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Colt_.45_(TV_series)" title="Colt .45 (TV series)">Colt .45</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas194_160-1"><a href="#cite_note-thomas194-160">[160]</a></sup> The success of these series helped to make up for losses in the film business.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas194_160-2"><a href="#cite_note-thomas194-160">[160]</a></sup> As a result, Jack decided to emphasize television production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas195_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas195-161">[161]</a></sup> Warner's produced a series of popular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Private_detective" title="Private detective">private detective</a> shows beginning with <i><a href="/wiki/77_Sunset_Strip" title="77 Sunset Strip">77 Sunset Strip</a></i> (1958–1964) followed by <i><a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_Eye" title="Hawaiian Eye">Hawaiian Eye</a></i> (1959–1963), <i><a href="/wiki/Bourbon_Street_Beat" title="Bourbon Street Beat">Bourbon Street Beat</a></i> (1960) and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surfside_Six" title="Surfside Six">Surfside Six</a></i> (1960–1962).</p>
<p>Within a few years, the studio provoked hostility among their TV stars such as <a href="/wiki/Clint_Walker" title="Clint Walker">Clint Walker</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_Garner" title="James Garner">James Garner</a>, who sued over a contract dispute<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas196-198_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas196-198-162">[162]</a></sup> and won. <a href="/wiki/Edd_Byrnes" title="Edd Byrnes">Edd Byrnes</a> was not so lucky and bought himself out of his contract. Jack was angered by their perceived ingratitude, who evidently showed more independence than film actors, deepening his contempt for the new medium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas199_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas199-163">[163]</a></sup> Many of Warner's television stars appeared in the casts of Warner's cinema releases. In 1963 a court decision forced Warner's to end contracts with their television stars, engaging them for specific series or film roles.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In the same year <a href="/wiki/Jack_Webb" title="Jack Webb">Jack Webb</a> took over the television unit without success.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dean_Martin_Frank_Sinatra_Dean_Martin_Show_1958.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="741" data-file-width="579" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Dean_Martin_Frank_Sinatra_Dean_Martin_Show_1958.JPG/100px-Dean_Martin_Frank_Sinatra_Dean_Martin_Show_1958.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Dean_Martin_Frank_Sinatra_Dean_Martin_Show_1958.JPG/150px-Dean_Martin_Frank_Sinatra_Dean_Martin_Show_1958.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Dean_Martin_Frank_Sinatra_Dean_Martin_Show_1958.JPG/200px-Dean_Martin_Frank_Sinatra_Dean_Martin_Show_1958.JPG 2x" width="100"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dean_Martin_Frank_Sinatra_Dean_Martin_Show_1958.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Dean_Martin" title="Dean Martin">Dean Martin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frank_Sinatra" title="Frank Sinatra">Frank Sinatra</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Warner Bros. was already the owner of extensive music-publishing holdings, whose tunes had appeared in countless cartoons (arranged by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Stalling" title="Carl Stalling">Carl Stalling</a>) and television shows (arranged by <a href="/wiki/Max_Steiner" title="Max Steiner">Max Steiner</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup>).</p>
<p>In 1958, the studio launched <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros._Records" title="Warner Bros. Records">Warner Bros. Records</a>. Initially the label released recordings made by their television stars—whether they could sing or not—and records based on television soundtracks.</p>
<p>In 1963, Warner agreed to a "rescue takeover" of <a href="/wiki/Frank_Sinatra" title="Frank Sinatra">Frank Sinatra</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Reprise_Records" title="Reprise Records">Reprise Records</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas255_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas255-165">[165]</a></sup> The deal gave Sinatra US$1.5 million and part ownership of Warner Bros. Records, making Reprise a sub-label.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas255_165-1"><a href="#cite_note-thomas255-165">[165]</a></sup> Most significantly the deal brought Reprise manager <a href="/wiki/Mo_Ostin" title="Mo Ostin">Morris "Mo" Ostin</a> into the company. In 1964, upon seeing the profits record companies made from Warner film music, Warner decided to claim ownership of the studio's film soundtracks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas265_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas265-166">[166]</a></sup> In its first eighteen months, Warner Bros. Records lost around $2 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas2645_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas2645-167">[167]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_owners">New owners</span></h3>
<p>Warner Bros. rebounded in the late 1950s, specializing in adaptations of popular plays like <i><a href="/wiki/The_Bad_Seed_(1956_film)" title="The Bad Seed (1956 film)">The Bad Seed</a></i> (1956), <i><a href="/wiki/No_Time_for_Sergeants_(1958_film)" title="No Time for Sergeants (1958 film)">No Time for Sergeants</a></i> (1958), and <i><a href="/wiki/Gypsy_(1962_film)" title="Gypsy (1962 film)">Gypsy</a></i> (1962).</p>
<p>While he slowly recovered from a car crash that occurred while vacationing in France in 1958, Jack returned to the studio and made sure his name was featured in studio press releases. From 1961-63, the studio's annual net profit was a little over $7 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H:325_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-H:325-168">[168]</a></sup> Warner paid an unprecedented $5.5 million for the film rights to the Broadway musical <i><a href="/wiki/My_Fair_Lady" title="My Fair Lady">My Fair Lady</a></i> in February 1962. The previous owner, <a href="/wiki/CBS" title="CBS">CBS</a> director <a href="/wiki/William_S._Paley" title="William S. Paley">William S. Paley</a>, set terms including half the distributor's gross profits "plus ownership of the negative at the end of the contract."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas259_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas259-169">[169]</a></sup> In 1963, the studio's net profit dropped to $3.7 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H:325_168-1"><a href="#cite_note-H:325-168">[168]</a></sup> By the mid-1960s, motion picture production was in decline, as the industry was in the midst of a painful transition from the <a href="/wiki/Classical_Hollywood_cinema" title="Classical Hollywood cinema">Golden Age of Hollywood</a> to the era now known as <a href="/wiki/New_Hollywood" title="New Hollywood">New Hollywood</a>. Few studio films were made in favor of co-productions (for which Warner provided facilities, money and distribution), and pickups of independent pictures.</p>
<p>With the success of the studio's 1964 film of Broadway play <i><a href="/wiki/My_Fair_Lady" title="My Fair Lady">My Fair Lady</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas2645_167-1"><a href="#cite_note-thomas2645-167">[167]</a></sup> as well as its soundtrack,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas2645_167-2"><a href="#cite_note-thomas2645-167">[167]</a></sup> Warner Bros. Records became a profitable subsidiary. The 1966 film <i><a href="/wiki/Who%27s_Afraid_of_Virginia_Woolf%3F_(film)" title="Who's Afraid of Virginia Woolf? (film)">Who's Afraid Of Virginia Woolf?</a></i> was a huge success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas278_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas278-170">[170]</a></sup></p>
<p>In November 1966, Jack gave in to advancing age and changing times,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas280_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas280-171">[171]</a></sup> selling control of the studio and music business to <a href="/wiki/Seven_Arts_Productions" title="Seven Arts Productions">Seven Arts Productions</a>, run by Canadian investors Elliot and Kenneth Hyman, for $32 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas279_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas279-172">[172]</a></sup> The company, including the studio, was renamed <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros.-Seven_Arts" title="Warner Bros.-Seven Arts">Warner Bros.-Seven Arts</a>. Warner remained president until the summer of 1967, when <i><a href="/wiki/Camelot_(film)" title="Camelot (film)">Camelot</a></i> failed at the box office and Warner gave up his position to his longtime publicity director, Ben Kalmenson;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas279280_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas279280-173">[173]</a></sup> Warner remained on board as an independent producer and vice-president.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas279_172-1"><a href="#cite_note-thomas279-172">[172]</a></sup> With the 1967 success of <i><a href="/wiki/Bonnie_and_Clyde_(film)" title="Bonnie and Clyde (film)">Bonnie and Clyde</a></i>, Warner Bros. was again profitable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas288_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas288-174">[174]</a></sup></p>
<p>Two years later the Hymans were tired and fed up with Jack Warner and his actions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas288_174-1"><a href="#cite_note-thomas288-174">[174]</a></sup> They accepted a cash-and-stock offer from <a href="/wiki/Kinney_National_Company" title="Kinney National Company">Kinney National Company</a> for more than $64 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas288_174-2"><a href="#cite_note-thomas288-174">[174]</a></sup> Kinney owned a Hollywood talent agency, <a href="/wiki/Ashley-Famous" title="Ashley-Famous">Ashley-Famous</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> whose founder <a href="/wiki/Ted_Ashley" title="Ted Ashley">Ted Ashley</a> led Kinney head <a href="/wiki/Steve_Ross_(Time_Warner_CEO)" title="Steve Ross (Time Warner CEO)">Steve Ross</a> to purchase Warner Bros. Ashley became the studio head and changed the name to Warner Bros., Inc. once again. Jack Warner was outraged by the Hymans' sale, and decided to retire, until his death from serious health complications of heart inflammation in August 1978.</p>
<p>Although movie audiences had shrunk, Warner's new management believed in the drawing power of stars, signing co-production deals with several of the biggest names of the day, including <a href="/wiki/Paul_Newman" title="Paul Newman">Paul Newman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Redford" title="Robert Redford">Robert Redford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barbra_Streisand" title="Barbra Streisand">Barbra Streisand</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Clint_Eastwood" title="Clint Eastwood">Clint Eastwood</a>, carrying the studio successfully through the 1970s and 1980s. Warner Bros. also made major profits on films and television shows built around the characters of <a href="/wiki/Superman" title="Superman">Superman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Batman" title="Batman">Batman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wonder_Woman" title="Wonder Woman">Wonder Woman</a> owned by Warner Bros. subsidiary <a href="/wiki/DC_Comics" title="DC Comics">DC Comics</a>.</p>
<p>Abandoning parking lots and funeral homes, the refocused Kinney renamed itself in honor of its best-known holding, <a href="/wiki/Warner_Communications" title="Warner Communications">Warner Communications</a>. Throughout the 1970s and 1980s Warner Communications branched out into other business, such as <a href="/wiki/Video_game" title="Video game">video game</a> company <a href="/wiki/Atari,_Inc." title="Atari, Inc.">Atari, Inc.</a> in 1976, and later the <a href="/wiki/Six_Flags" title="Six Flags">Six Flags</a> theme parks.</p>
<p><span id="Columbia_partnership"></span>From 1971 until the end of 1987, Warner's international distribution operations were a joint venture with <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Pictures" title="Columbia Pictures">Columbia Pictures</a>. In some countries, this joint venture distributed films from other companies (such as <a href="/wiki/EMI_Films" title="EMI Films">EMI Films</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Cannon_Group" title="The Cannon Group">Cannon Films</a> in the UK). Warner ended the venture in 1988 and partnered with <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Pictures" title="Walt Disney Pictures">Walt Disney Pictures</a>. This joint venture lasted until 1993, when Disney created Buena Vista International.</p>
<p>In 1972, in a cost-cutting move, Warner and Columbia formed a third company called The Burbank Studios (TBS).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bingen_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bingen-176">[176]</a></sup> They would share the Warner lot in Burbank.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bingen_176-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bingen-176">[176]</a></sup> Both studios technically became production entities, giving TBS day-to-day responsibility for studio grounds and upkeep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bingen_176-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bingen-176">[176]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Ranch" title="Columbia Ranch">Columbia Ranch</a> (about a mile north of Warner's lot) was part of the deal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bingen_176-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bingen-176">[176]</a></sup> The Warner-Columbia relationship was acrimonious, but the reluctance of both studios to approve or spend money on capital upgrades that might only help the other did have the unintended consequence of preserving the Warner lot's primary function as a filmmaking facility while it produced relatively little during the 1970s and 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bingen_176-4"><a href="#cite_note-Bingen-176">[176]</a></sup> (Most films produced after 1968 were filmed on location after the failure of <i>Camelot</i> was partially attributed to the fact it was set in England but obviously filmed in Burbank.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bingen_176-5"><a href="#cite_note-Bingen-176">[176]</a></sup> With control over its own lot tied up in TBS, Warner ultimately retained a significant portion of its <a href="/wiki/Backlot" title="Backlot">backlot</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bingen_176-6"><a href="#cite_note-Bingen-176">[176]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/20th_Century_Fox" title="20th Century Fox">Fox</a> sold its backlot to create <a href="/wiki/Century_City,_Los_Angeles" title="Century City, Los Angeles">Century City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Universal_Studios" title="Universal Studios">Universal</a> turned part of its backlot into <a href="/wiki/Universal_Studios_Hollywood" title="Universal Studios Hollywood">a theme park</a> and <a href="/wiki/Universal_CityWalk" title="Universal CityWalk">shopping center</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Studios_(Burbank)" title="Walt Disney Studios (Burbank)">Disney replaced its backlot with office buildings</a> and exiled its <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Animation_Studios" title="Walt Disney Animation Studios">animation department</a> to an industrial park in Glendale.</p>
<p>In 1989, a solution to the situation became evident when Warner Bros. acquired <a href="/wiki/Lorimar-Telepictures" title="Lorimar-Telepictures">Lorimar-Telepictures</a> and gained control of the former MGM studio lot in <a href="/wiki/Culver_City,_California" title="Culver City, California">Culver City</a>, and that same year, Sony bought Columbia Pictures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bingen_176-7"><a href="#cite_note-Bingen-176">[176]</a></sup> Sony was flush with cash and Warner Bros. now had two studio lots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bingen_176-8"><a href="#cite_note-Bingen-176">[176]</a></sup> In 1990, TBS ended when Sony bought the MGM lot from Warner and moved Columbia to Culver City.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bingen_176-9"><a href="#cite_note-Bingen-176">[176]</a></sup> However, Warner kept the Columbia Ranch, now known as the Warner Bros. Ranch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bingen_176-10"><a href="#cite_note-Bingen-176">[176]</a></sup></p>
<p>Warner Communications merged in 1989 with <a href="/wiki/White_shoe_firm" title="White shoe firm">white-shoe</a> publishing company <a href="/wiki/Time_Inc." title="Time Inc.">Time Inc.</a> Time claimed a higher level of prestige, while Warner Bros. provided the profits. The <a href="/wiki/Time_Warner" title="Time Warner">Time Warner</a> merger was almost derailed when <a href="/wiki/Gulf_and_Western_Industries" title="Gulf and Western Industries">Paramount Communications</a> (Formerly <a href="/wiki/Gulf_and_Western_Industries" title="Gulf and Western Industries">Gulf+Western</a>, later sold to <a href="/wiki/Viacom" title="Viacom">Viacom</a>), launched a $12.2 billion <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hostile_takeover" title="Hostile takeover">hostile takeover</a> bid for Time Inc., forcing Time to acquire Warner with a $14.9 billion cash/stock offer. Paramount responded with a lawsuit filed in <a href="/wiki/Delaware" title="Delaware">Delaware</a> court to break up the merger. Paramount lost and the merger proceeded.</p>
<p>In 1992, <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros._Family_Entertainment" title="Warner Bros. Family Entertainment">Warner Bros. Family Entertainment</a> was established to produce various family-oriented films.</p>
<p>In 1997, Time Warner sold Six Flags. The takeover of Time Warner in 2000 by then-high-flying <a href="/wiki/AOL" title="AOL">AOL</a> did not prove a good match, and following the collapse in "dot-com" stocks, the AOL element was banished from the corporate name.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Since_1995">Since 1995</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WarnerStudio.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1717" data-file-width="2576" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/WarnerStudio.jpg/220px-WarnerStudio.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/WarnerStudio.jpg/330px-WarnerStudio.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/WarnerStudio.jpg/440px-WarnerStudio.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WarnerStudio.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A panoramic view over today's studio premises.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1995, Warner and station owner <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tribune_Company" title="Tribune Company">Tribune Company</a> of Chicago launched <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_WB_Television_Network" title="The WB Television Network">The WB Network</a>, seeking a niche market in teenagers. The WB's early programming included an abundance of teenage fare, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Buffy_the_Vampire_Slayer" title="Buffy the Vampire Slayer">Buffy the Vampire Slayer</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Smallville" title="Smallville">Smallville</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Dawson%27s_Creek" title="Dawson's Creek">Dawson's Creek</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/One_Tree_Hill_(TV_series)" title="One Tree Hill (TV series)">One Tree Hill</a></i>. Two dramas produced by <a href="/wiki/Spelling_Television" title="Spelling Television">Spelling Television</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/7th_Heaven_(TV_series)" title="7th Heaven (TV series)">7th Heaven</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Charmed" title="Charmed">Charmed</a>,</i> helped bring The WB into the spotlight. <i>Charmed</i> lasted eight seasons, becoming the longest running drama with female leads. <i>7th Heaven</i> ran for eleven seasons and was the longest running family drama and longest-running show for the network. In 2006, Warner and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CBS_Paramount_Television" title="CBS Paramount Television">CBS Paramount Television</a> decided to close The WB and CBS's <a href="/wiki/UPN" title="UPN">UPN</a> and jointly launch <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_CW_Television_Network" title="The CW Television Network">The CW Television Network</a>.</p>
<p>In 1998, Warner Bros. celebrated its 75th anniversary. In 1999, Terry Semels and Robert Daly resigned as studio heads after a career 13 Oscar nominated films. Daly and Semels were said to have popularized the modern model of partner financing and profit sharing for film production. Until mid-1999, when Alan Horn and Barry Meyer replaced Daly and Semester as new studio heads, in which the studio had continued success in movies, Television shows, cartoons, that the previous studio heads had for the studio.</p>
<p>In the late 1990s, Warner obtained rights to the <i><a href="/wiki/Harry_Potter" title="Harry Potter">Harry Potter</a></i> novels and released feature film adaptations of the <a href="/wiki/Harry_Potter_and_the_Philosopher%27s_Stone_(film)" title="Harry Potter and the Philosopher's Stone (film)">first</a> in 2001. Subsequently, they released the <a href="/wiki/Harry_Potter_and_the_Chamber_of_Secrets_(film)" title="Harry Potter and the Chamber of Secrets (film)">second film</a> in 2002, the <a href="/wiki/Harry_Potter_and_the_Prisoner_of_Azkaban_(film)" title="Harry Potter and the Prisoner of Azkaban (film)">third</a> in June 2004, the <a href="/wiki/Harry_Potter_and_the_Goblet_of_Fire_(film)" title="Harry Potter and the Goblet of Fire (film)">fourth</a> in November 2005, the <a href="/wiki/Harry_Potter_and_the_Order_of_the_Phoenix_(film)" title="Harry Potter and the Order of the Phoenix (film)">fifth</a> in July 2007, and the <a href="/wiki/Harry_Potter_and_the_Half-Blood_Prince_(film)" title="Harry Potter and the Half-Blood Prince (film)">sixth</a> in July 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> The seventh (and at that time, final) book was released as two movies; <a href="/wiki/Harry_Potter_and_the_Deathly_Hallows_%E2%80%93_Part_1" title="Harry Potter and the Deathly Hallows – Part 1">Deathly Hallows - Part 1</a> in November 2010 and <a href="/wiki/Harry_Potter_and_the_Deathly_Hallows_%E2%80%93_Part_2" title="Harry Potter and the Deathly Hallows – Part 2">Deathly Hallows - Part 2</a> in July 2011.</p>
<p>In 2004, Warner Bros. became the first studio in history to gross more than $2 billion internationally in a single year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-178">[178]</a></sup></p>
<p>From 2006, Warner Bros. operated a joint venture with <a href="/wiki/China_Film_Group_Corporation" title="China Film Group Corporation">China Film Group Corporation</a> and HG to form <b>Warner China Film HG</b> to produce films in Hong Kong and China, including <a href="/wiki/Connected_(film)" title="Connected (film)"><i>Connected</i></a>, a remake of the 2004 <a href="/wiki/Thriller_film" title="Thriller film">thriller film</a> <a href="/wiki/Cellular_(film)" title="Cellular (film)"><i>Cellular</i></a><i>.</i> They co-produced many other Chinese films.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Warner Bros. played a large part in the discontinuation of the <a href="/wiki/HD_DVD" title="HD DVD">HD DVD</a> format. On January 4, 2008, Warner Bros. announced that they would drop support of HD DVD in favor of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blu-ray_Disc" title="Blu-ray Disc">Blu-ray Disc</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> HD DVDs continued to be released through May 2008, but only following Blu-ray and DVD releases.</p>
<p>In 2009, Warner Bros. set studio and industry records, raking in $2.13 billion in domestic receipts and $4.01 billion worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_178-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-178">[178]</a></sup></p>
<p>Warner Bros.' <a href="/wiki/Harry_Potter_(film_series)" title="Harry Potter (film series)"><i>Harry Potter</i> film series</a> was the worldwide highest-grossing film series of all time without adjusting for inflation. Its <a href="/wiki/Batman_in_film" title="Batman in film"><i>Batman</i> film series</a> was one of only two series to have two entries earn more than $1 billion worldwide. <i><a href="/wiki/Harry_Potter_and_the_Deathly_Hallows_%E2%80%93_Part_2" title="Harry Potter and the Deathly Hallows – Part 2">Harry Potter and the Deathly Hallows – Part 2</a></i> was Warner Bros.' highest-grossing movie ever (surpassing <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dark_Knight_(film)" title="The Dark Knight (film)">The Dark Knight</a></i>).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> However, the Harry Potter movies have produced a net loss due to <a href="/wiki/Hollywood_accounting" title="Hollywood accounting">Hollywood accounting</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/IMAX_Corporation" title="IMAX Corporation">IMAX Corp.signed</a> with Warner Bros. Pictures in April 2010 to release as many as 20 giant-format films through 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup></p>
<p>Warner Bros. formed a short form digital unit, Blue Ribbon Content, under its <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros._Animation" title="Warner Bros. Animation">Warner Bros. Animation</a> &amp; Warner Digital Series president.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup></p>
<p>On February 6, 2014, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Columbia_TriStar_Warner_Filmes_de_Portugal_Ltda.&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Columbia TriStar Warner Filmes de Portugal Ltda. (page does not exist)">Columbia TriStar Warner Filmes de Portugal Ltda.</a>, a joint venture with <a href="/wiki/Sony_Pictures" title="Sony Pictures">Sony Pictures</a> which distributed both companies' films in Portugal, announced that it would have its offices no more from March 31, 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lusomundo" title="Lusomundo">NOS Audiovisuais</a> is handling the distribution of Warner Bros. films in Portugal since then.</p>
<p>As of 2015, Warner Bros. is one of only three studios to have released a pair of billion-dollar films in the same year (along with <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Studios_Motion_Pictures" title="Walt Disney Studios Motion Pictures">Walt Disney Studios Motion Pictures</a> and <a href="/wiki/Universal_Studios" title="Universal Studios">Universal Studios</a>); the distinction was achieved in 2012 with <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dark_Knight_Rises" title="The Dark Knight Rises">The Dark Knight Rises</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Hobbit:_An_Unexpected_Journey" title="The Hobbit: An Unexpected Journey">The Hobbit: An Unexpected Journey</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> As of 2016, it is only studio to cross $1 billion at the domestic box-office every year since 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production_deals">Production deals</span></h2>

<li><a href="/wiki/Appian_Way_Productions" title="Appian Way Productions">Appian Way Productions</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Greg_Berlanti" title="Greg Berlanti">Berlanti Productions</a> (2010–)</li>

<li><a href="/wiki/RatPac-Dune_Entertainment" title="RatPac-Dune Entertainment">RatPac-Dune Entertainment</a> (2014–)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup></li>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Acquired_libraries">Acquired libraries</span></h3>
<p>Mergers and acquisitions have helped Warner Bros. accumulate a diverse collection of films, cartoons and television programs.</p>
<p>In the aftermath of the 1948 antitrust suit, uncertain times led Warner Bros. in 1956 to sell most of its pre-1950<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ymrt_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-ymrt-146">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aap_exceptions_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-aap_exceptions-147">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archive.org_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-archive.org-148">[148]</a></sup> films and cartoons to the now-defunct holding company <a href="/wiki/Associated_Artists_Productions" title="Associated Artists Productions">Associated Artists Productions</a> (a.a.p.). In addition, a.a.p. also obtained the <a href="/wiki/Fleischer_Studios" title="Fleischer Studios">Fleischer Studios</a> and <a href="/wiki/Famous_Studios" title="Famous Studios">Famous Studios</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Popeye" title="Popeye">Popeye</a></i> cartoons, originally from <a href="/wiki/Paramount_Pictures" title="Paramount Pictures">Paramount Pictures</a>. Two years later, a.a.p. was sold to <a href="/wiki/United_Artists" title="United Artists">United Artists</a>, which held them until 1981, when <a href="/wiki/Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer" title="Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer">Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer</a> acquired United Artists.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1982, during their independent years, <a href="/wiki/Turner_Broadcasting_System" title="Turner Broadcasting System">Turner Broadcasting System</a> acquired Brut Productions, the film production arm of <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>-based then-struggling personal-care company <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Faberge_Inc." title="Faberge Inc.">Faberge Inc.</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1986, Turner Broadcasting System, by then having failed to acquire Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer, settled for ownership of the Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer/United Artists library. This included almost all of the pre-May 1986 MGM film and television libraries with the exception of the films &amp; television series owned by United Artists (i.e. James Bond franchise), although some United Artists material were included such as the a.a.p. library, <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North American</a> rights to a majority of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RKO_Radio_Pictures" title="RKO Radio Pictures">RKO Radio Pictures</a> library and the television series <i><a href="/wiki/Gilligan%27s_Island" title="Gilligan's Island">Gilligan's Island</a></i>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In 1989, Warner Communications acquired the library of film and television company <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lorimar" title="Lorimar">Lorimar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merv_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-Merv-196">[196]</a></sup> This acquisition included the 1974-1989 library of <a href="/wiki/Rankin/Bass_Productions" title="Rankin/Bass Productions">Rankin/Bass Productions</a>, as well as the post-1947 <a href="/wiki/Monogram_Pictures" title="Monogram Pictures">Monogram Pictures</a>/<a href="/wiki/Monogram_Pictures" title="Monogram Pictures">Allied Artists Pictures Corporation</a> and 1936-1946 <a href="/wiki/Monogram_Pictures" title="Monogram Pictures">Monogram Pictures</a> libraries, which the Lorimar library included.</p>
<p>In 1990, Turner Broadcasting System acquired animation studio <a href="/wiki/Hanna-Barbera" title="Hanna-Barbera">Hanna-Barbera</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ruby-Spears" title="Ruby-Spears">Ruby-Spears</a> from <a href="/wiki/Taft_Broadcasting" title="Taft Broadcasting">Taft Broadcasting</a>, and years later, Turner Broadcasting System acquired <a href="/wiki/Castle_Rock_Entertainment" title="Castle Rock Entertainment">Castle Rock Entertainment</a> on December 22, 1993<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup> and even <a href="/wiki/New_Line_Cinema" title="New Line Cinema">New Line Cinema</a> on January 28, 1994.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> On October 10, 1996, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TimeWarner" title="TimeWarner">TimeWarner</a> acquired Turner Broadcasting System, thus bringing the pre-1950 silent/sound film and pre-August 1948 cartoon libraries back home. However, Castle Rock Entertainment's pre-1994 library is currently owned by <a href="/wiki/Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer" title="Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer">Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer</a>, and as such, Warner Bros. owns Castle Rock Entertainment's post-1994 library.</p>
<p>On October 4, 2007, Warner Bros. added the <i><a href="/wiki/Peanuts" title="Peanuts">Peanuts</a></i> library to its under license from <a href="/wiki/Peanuts#Peanuts_Worldwide.2C_LLC" title="Peanuts">Peanuts Worldwide, LLC</a>, licensor and owner of the <i>Peanuts</i> material; this includes all of the television library outside of the theatrical library, which continues to be a joint venture between <a href="/wiki/CBS_Corporation" title="CBS Corporation">CBS Corporation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paramount_Pictures" title="Paramount Pictures">Paramount Pictures</a>, both companies part of <a href="/wiki/National_Amusements" title="National Amusements">National Amusements</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2008, Warner Bros. shut down New Line Cinema as an independent mini-major studio, so they added the New Line Cinema film and television collections to their library. The next year, on October 15, 2009, Warner Bros. acquired home video distribution rights to the <i><a href="/wiki/Sesame_Street" title="Sesame Street">Sesame Street</a></i> library, in conjunction with <a href="/wiki/Sesame_Workshop" title="Sesame Workshop">Sesame Workshop</a>. In June 2016, it created the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Harry_Potter_Global_Franchise_Development_Team&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Harry Potter Global Franchise Development Team (page does not exist)">Harry Potter Global Franchise Development Team</a> (HPGFDT Group) to oversee it ownership of the Harry Potter franchise worldwide (including trademark to Wizarding World)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Warner_Bros._Archives">The Warner Bros. Archives</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Southern_California" title="University of Southern California">University of Southern California</a> Warner Bros. Archives is the largest single studio collection in the world. Donated in 1977 to USC's School of Cinema-Television by Warner Communications, the WBA houses departmental records that detail Warner Bros. activities from the studio's first major feature, <i>My Four Years in Germany</i> (1918), to its sale to Seven Arts in 1968. It presents a complete view of the production process during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Golden_Age_of_Hollywood" title="Golden Age of Hollywood">Golden Age of Hollywood</a>. UA donated pre-1950 Warner Bros. nitrate negatives to the <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress" title="Library of Congress">Library of Congress</a> and post-1951 negatives to the <a href="/wiki/UCLA_Film_and_Television_Archive" title="UCLA Film and Television Archive">UCLA Film and Television Archive</a>. Most of the company's legal files, scripts, and production materials were donated to the <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin_Center_for_Film_and_Theater_Research" title="Wisconsin Center for Film and Theater Research">Wisconsin Center for Film and Theater Research</a>.</p>
